# Problem 3226

- Domain: `coding`

## Problem

```text
You are given a 0-indexed integer array nums of even length and there is also an empty array arr. Alice and Bob decided to play a game where in every round Alice and Bob will do one move. The rules of the game are as follows:

Every round, first Alice will remove the minimum element from nums, and then Bob does the same.
Now, first Bob will append the removed element in the array arr, and then Alice does the same.
The game continues until nums becomes empty.

Return the resulting array arr.
 
Example 1:

Input: nums = [5,4,2,3]
Output: [3,2,5,4]
Explanation: In round one, first Alice removes 2 and then Bob removes 3. Then in arr firstly Bob appends 3 and then Alice appends 2. So arr = [3,2].
At the begining of round two, nums = [5,4]. Now, first Alice removes 4 and then Bob removes 5. Then both append in arr which becomes [3,2,5,4].

Example 2:

Input: nums = [2,5]
Output: [5,2]
Explanation: In round one, first Alice removes 2 and then Bob removes 5. Then in arr firstly Bob appends and then Alice appends. So arr = [5,2].

 
Constraints:

1 <= nums.length <= 100
1 <= nums[i] <= 100
nums.length % 2 == 0
```

### Starter Code

```python
class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
```

## Generation

### System Prompt

```text
You are an expert Python programmer. You will be given a question (problem specification) and will generate a correct Python program that matches the specification and passes all tests.
```

### User Prompt

```text
### Question:
You are given a 0-indexed integer array nums of even length and there is also an empty array arr. Alice and Bob decided to play a game where in every round Alice and Bob will do one move. The rules of the game are as follows:

Every round, first Alice will remove the minimum element from nums, and then Bob does the same.
Now, first Bob will append the removed element in the array arr, and then Alice does the same.
The game continues until nums becomes empty.

Return the resulting array arr.
 
Example 1:

Input: nums = [5,4,2,3]
Output: [3,2,5,4]
Explanation: In round one, first Alice removes 2 and then Bob removes 3. Then in arr firstly Bob appends 3 and then Alice appends 2. So arr = [3,2].
At the begining of round two, nums = [5,4]. Now, first Alice removes 4 and then Bob removes 5. Then both append in arr which becomes [3,2,5,4].

Example 2:

Input: nums = [2,5]
Output: [5,2]
Explanation: In round one, first Alice removes 2 and then Bob removes 5. Then in arr firstly Bob appends and then Alice appends. So arr = [5,2].

 
Constraints:

1 <= nums.length <= 100
1 <= nums[i] <= 100
nums.length % 2 == 0

### Format: You will use the following starter code to write the solution to the problem and enclose your code within delimiters.
```python
class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        
```

### Answer: (use the provided format with backticks)
```

### Outputs

<details>
<summary>Generation 1</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        ans = []
        # nums length is even. Take elements in pairs: smallest, next smallest.
        # Append each pair as: second (Alice's removed next) then first.
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            ans.append(nums[i + 1])
            ans.append(nums[i])
        return ans
```
```

</details>

```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # For each pair (small, large): append (large, small)
        res = []
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # Pair up consecutive elements after sorting:
        # arr gets the larger from each pair first, then the smaller.
        res = []
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        res = []
        # After sorting, pick pairs: for each pair (small, large),
        # append large first then small.
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

<details>
<summary>Generation 5</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        n = len(nums)
        res = [0] * n

        # Place elements from the sorted array:
        # For i in [0, n/2): Bob takes nums[i+n/2], Alice takes nums[n/2 - 1 - i]
        # This matches the order of appends into arr (Bob first, then Alice).
        for i in range(n // 2):
            res[2 * i] = nums[n // 2 + i]
            res[2 * i + 1] = nums[n // 2 - 1 - i]

        return res
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Generation 6</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # After sorting: take elements at odd indices first (the "second" smallest in each pair),
        # then elements at even indices.
        n = len(nums)
        res = []
        for i in range(1, n, 2):
            res.append(nums[i])
        for i in range(0, n, 2):
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```
```

</details>

from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        res = []
        # After sorting: Alice takes nums[0], Bob takes nums[1], etc.
        # In arr, Bob's removed element is appended first, then Alice's.
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        res = []
        # Take elements in pairs: (smallest, next smallest), output second then first
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res

from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # After sorting: for each pair (small, large), output large then small
        ans = []
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            ans.append(nums[i + 1])
            ans.append(nums[i])
        return ans
```

<details>
<summary>Generation 10</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List
import heapq

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        # Use a min-heap to repeatedly remove the smallest element
        heapq.heapify(nums)
        arr = []
        
        while nums:
            # Alice removes min
            a = heapq.heappop(nums)
            # Bob removes next min
            b = heapq.heappop(nums)
            # Bob appends first, then Alice
            arr.append(b)
            arr.append(a)
        
        return arr
```
```

### Prompt

```text
## Guidelines
You need to extract and summarize useful insights in the format of memory items based on comparing the successful and unsuccessful solution attempts below.
The goal of summarized memory items is to be GENERALIZABLE and TRANSFERABLE — each insight must be useful not just for this problem, but for a broad class of similar problems. Write each memory as advice that would help someone who has never seen this specific problem.

## Important notes
- You must first think about why certain attempts succeeded and others failed, then summarize the insights.
- You can extract multiple memory items from the comparison. Each should capture a distinct, independent insight.
- Prioritize GENERAL META-REASONING techniques over object-level hints. Favor principles about representation choice, constraint formalization, invariant selection, validation, decomposition, debugging, or algorithm selection.
- Convert failure observations into POSITIVE reusable guidance. Do not write memories that are just restatements of one wrong step.
- Do NOT mention specific values, answers, variable names, or problem-specific details. Every memory item must stand on its own as a general problem-solving principle or technique that transfers across different problems in the same domain.
- Avoid narrow one-off tricks unless they clearly transfer to a recurring class of problems. Fewer high-quality memories are better than many repetitive ones.
- Emphasize high-level strategies that would still make sense on a different problem in the same domain.

## Examples of good meta-reasoning memories
- Title: Constraint-First Reformulation
  Description: Turn informal requirements into explicit constraints before solving.
  Content: Rewrite vague conditions as equations, inequalities, parity rules, or state constraints. Use those formal constraints to guide the solution and reject invalid branches early.
- Title: Validate Risky Transformations
  Description: Check any step that can introduce invalid candidates against the original problem.
  Content: Squaring, dividing, relaxing constraints, or compressing state can create artifacts that look valid only in the transformed setting. Add a direct verification pass against the original conditions before committing to an answer.
- Title: Model the Smallest Faithful State
  Description: Reduce the problem to the minimal state that still determines future decisions.
  Content: If only parity, remainder class, last element, or turn matters, build the reasoning or DP around that reduced state instead of the full object. This shrinks search and makes transitions easier to verify.

### Problem
You are given a 0-indexed integer array nums of even length and there is also an empty array arr. Alice and Bob decided to play a game where in every round Alice and Bob will do one move. The rules of the game are as follows:

Every round, first Alice will remove the minimum element from nums, and then Bob does the same.
Now, first Bob will append the removed element in the array arr, and then Alice does the same.
The game continues until nums becomes empty.

Return the resulting array arr.
 
Example 1:

Input: nums = [5,4,2,3]
Output: [3,2,5,4]
Explanation: In round one, first Alice removes 2 and then Bob removes 3. Then in arr firstly Bob appends 3 and then Alice appends 2. So arr = [3,2].
At the begining of round two, nums = [5,4]. Now, first Alice removes 4 and then Bob removes 5. Then both append in arr which becomes [3,2,5,4].

Example 2:

Input: nums = [2,5]
Output: [5,2]
Explanation: In round one, first Alice removes 2 and then Bob removes 5. Then in arr firstly Bob appends and then Alice appends. So arr = [5,2].

 
Constraints:

1 <= nums.length <= 100
1 <= nums[i] <= 100
nums.length % 2 == 0

### Solutions
#### Correct
Attempt 1
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        ans = []
        # nums length is even. Take elements in pairs: smallest, next smallest.
        # Append each pair as: second (Alice's removed next) then first.
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            ans.append(nums[i + 1])
            ans.append(nums[i])
        return ans
```

Attempt 2
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # For each pair (small, large): append (large, small)
        res = []
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

Attempt 3
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # Pair up consecutive elements after sorting:
        # arr gets the larger from each pair first, then the smaller.
        res = []
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

Attempt 4
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        res = []
        # After sorting, pick pairs: for each pair (small, large),
        # append large first then small.
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

Attempt 5
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        res = []
        # After sorting: Alice takes nums[0], Bob takes nums[1], etc.
        # In arr, Bob's removed element is appended first, then Alice's.
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

```python

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        res = []
        # Take elements in pairs: (smallest, next smallest), output second then first
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

Attempt 7
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # After sorting: for each pair (small, large), output large then small
        ans = []
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            ans.append(nums[i + 1])
            ans.append(nums[i])
        return ans
```

Attempt 8
```python
from typing import List
import heapq

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        # Use a min-heap to repeatedly remove the smallest element
        heapq.heapify(nums)
        arr = []
        
        while nums:
            # Alice removes min
            a = heapq.heappop(nums)
            # Bob removes next min
            b = heapq.heappop(nums)
            # Bob appends first, then Alice
            arr.append(b)
            arr.append(a)
        
        return arr
```

#### Partially Correct
Attempt 1
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        n = len(nums)
        res = [0] * n

        # Place elements from the sorted array:
        # For i in [0, n/2): Bob takes nums[i+n/2], Alice takes nums[n/2 - 1 - i]
        # This matches the order of appends into arr (Bob first, then Alice).
        for i in range(n // 2):
            res[2 * i] = nums[n // 2 + i]
            res[2 * i + 1] = nums[n // 2 - 1 - i]

        return res
```

Attempt 2
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # After sorting: take elements at odd indices first (the "second" smallest in each pair),
        # then elements at even indices.
        n = len(nums)
        res = []
        for i in range(1, n, 2):
            res.append(nums[i])
        for i in range(0, n, 2):
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

### Parsed Memory Items

1. Sort + Pairwise Round Simulation
Description: After sorting, the game’s behavior depends only on repeatedly removing the smallest two remaining elements per round and outputting them in the round’s append order.
<details>
<summary>Content 1</summary>

```text
Sort the array so “remove minimum” becomes predictable as taking consecutive smallest elements. Then process the sorted array in pairs corresponding to each round’s two removals, appending in the exact order defined by who records first and who records second. This avoids trying to reason about the original unsorted dynamics.
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Reasoning 1</summary>

```text
The successful attempts all reduce the game to a simple transformation after sorting: repeatedly taking the two smallest remaining elements and outputting them in a specific order (the second removal first). They consistently maintain the crucial “who appends first” ordering relative to “who removes first” within each round. The partially correct attempts either correct the set of elements but mishandle the output order globally (e.g., outputting all elements from one index parity before the other, which breaks the interleaving produced by rounds) or use a more complex index-mapping approach that is easy to get subtly wrong. The heap approach succeeds because it directly simulates “remove minimum twice per round,” which avoids mistaken indexing assumptions.
```

</details>

2. Preserve Interleaving Order from Turn Rules
Description: When output order is determined by alternating turns, you must interleave results per round rather than group by index ranges.
<details>
<summary>Content 2</summary>

```text
Even if you pick the correct multiset of values, producing them in the wrong global sequence fails. Track the exact append sequence within each round (who appends first) and mirror that by appending two values immediately as a block (with the correct internal order) before moving to the next pair.
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Reasoning 2</summary>

```text
The successful attempts all reduce the game to a simple transformation after sorting: repeatedly taking the two smallest remaining elements and outputting them in a specific order (the second removal first). They consistently maintain the crucial “who appends first” ordering relative to “who removes first” within each round. The partially correct attempts either correct the set of elements but mishandle the output order globally (e.g., outputting all elements from one index parity before the other, which breaks the interleaving produced by rounds) or use a more complex index-mapping approach that is easy to get subtly wrong. The heap approach succeeds because it directly simulates “remove minimum twice per round,” which avoids mistaken indexing assumptions.
```

</details>

3. Validate Index-Mapping Against the Round Timeline
Description: Complex index formulas should be checked by mapping them back to the per-round removal/append timeline to prevent subtle ordering errors.
<details>
<summary>Content 3</summary>

```text
If you choose a direct indexing approach (writing into a preallocated result array), verify that each mapped position corresponds to the same element’s removal order and append order within its round. A quick timeline check—“first removal by Alice, second removal by Bob, then Bob appends before Alice”—catches off-by-one and swapping mistakes that grouping-based logic may miss.
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Reasoning 3</summary>

```text
The successful attempts all reduce the game to a simple transformation after sorting: repeatedly taking the two smallest remaining elements and outputting them in a specific order (the second removal first). They consistently maintain the crucial “who appends first” ordering relative to “who removes first” within each round. The partially correct attempts either correct the set of elements but mishandle the output order globally (e.g., outputting all elements from one index parity before the other, which breaks the interleaving produced by rounds) or use a more complex index-mapping approach that is easy to get subtly wrong. The heap approach succeeds because it directly simulates “remove minimum twice per round,” which avoids mistaken indexing assumptions.
```

</details>

4. Use Direct Simulation When Abstractions Are Risky
Description: If deriving a closed-form transformation risks ordering mistakes, a direct simulation with a priority structure can be more reliable.
<details>
<summary>Content 4</summary>

```text
A min-heap (or equivalent) cleanly models “repeatedly remove current minimum” without relying on fragile index reasoning. This approach works especially well when the operation sequence is fixed (e.g., remove twice per round and append in a known order), providing a correctness baseline for comparison.
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Reasoning 4</summary>

```text
The successful attempts all reduce the game to a simple transformation after sorting: repeatedly taking the two smallest remaining elements and outputting them in a specific order (the second removal first). They consistently maintain the crucial “who appends first” ordering relative to “who removes first” within each round. The partially correct attempts either correct the set of elements but mishandle the output order globally (e.g., outputting all elements from one index parity before the other, which breaks the interleaving produced by rounds) or use a more complex index-mapping approach that is easy to get subtly wrong. The heap approach succeeds because it directly simulates “remove minimum twice per round,” which avoids mistaken indexing assumptions.
```

</details>

<memories>
<memory>
<title>Sort + Pairwise Round Simulation</title>
<description>After sorting, the game’s behavior depends only on repeatedly removing the smallest two remaining elements per round and outputting them in the round’s append order.</description>
<content>Sort the array so “remove minimum” becomes predictable as taking consecutive smallest elements. Then process the sorted array in pairs corresponding to each round’s two removals, appending in the exact order defined by who records first and who records second. This avoids trying to reason about the original unsorted dynamics.</content>
</memory>

### Baseline

### System Prompt

```text
You are an expert Python programmer. You will be given a question (problem specification) and will generate a correct Python program that matches the specification and passes all tests.
```

### User Prompt

```text
### Question:
You are given a 0-indexed integer array nums of even length and there is also an empty array arr. Alice and Bob decided to play a game where in every round Alice and Bob will do one move. The rules of the game are as follows:

Every round, first Alice will remove the minimum element from nums, and then Bob does the same.
Now, first Bob will append the removed element in the array arr, and then Alice does the same.
The game continues until nums becomes empty.

Return the resulting array arr.
 
Example 1:

Input: nums = [5,4,2,3]
Output: [3,2,5,4]
Explanation: In round one, first Alice removes 2 and then Bob removes 3. Then in arr firstly Bob appends 3 and then Alice appends 2. So arr = [3,2].
At the begining of round two, nums = [5,4]. Now, first Alice removes 4 and then Bob removes 5. Then both append in arr which becomes [3,2,5,4].

Example 2:

Input: nums = [2,5]
Output: [5,2]
Explanation: In round one, first Alice removes 2 and then Bob removes 5. Then in arr firstly Bob appends and then Alice appends. So arr = [5,2].

 
Constraints:

1 <= nums.length <= 100
1 <= nums[i] <= 100
nums.length % 2 == 0

### Format: You will use the following starter code to write the solution to the problem and enclose your code within delimiters.
```python
class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        
```

### Answer: (use the provided format with backticks)
```

#### Outputs

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 1</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        n = len(nums)
        # nums are paired as (smallest, second smallest, ...)
        # Output takes second smallest of each pair first, then smallest of each pair.
        res = []
        for i in range(1, n, 2):
            res.append(nums[i])
        for i in range(0, n, 2):
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 2</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # Take from the middle to the end in descending order, then from the start to the middle in descending order
        n = len(nums)
        half = n // 2
        return nums[half:][::-1] + nums[:half][::-1]
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 3</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        res = []
        # Take pairs from smallest side, output as [second_smallest, smallest] per pair
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```

<summary>Baseline generation 4</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # After sorting: Alice takes smallest at each round, Bob takes next smallest.
        # arr gets Bob's removed first, then Alice's removed.
        res = []
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])  # Bob's element
            res.append(nums[i])      # Alice's element
        return res
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 5</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List
import heapq

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        # Use a min-heap to always remove the minimum element.
        heapq.heapify(nums)
        res = []
        
        # Each round consumes 2 numbers: Alice then Bob.
        while nums:
            a = heapq.heappop(nums)  # removed by Alice
            b = heapq.heappop(nums)  # removed by Bob
            # Bob appends first, then Alice.
            res.append(b)
            res.append(a)
        
        return res
```
```

</details>

        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):

```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        res = []
        # After sorting: Alice removes 2nd,4th,... (index 1,3,5...) (smaller half)
        # Bob removes 1st,3rd,... (index 0,2,4...) (larger half) and Bob appends first.
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i+1])  # Bob's removed element (larger)
            res.append(nums[i])    # Alice's removed element (smaller)
        return res
```
```

```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        res = []
        # After sorting, take pairs (smallest with next smallest).
        # For each pair [a, b] (a < b): Bob takes b first, then Alice takes a.
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```
```

<summary>Baseline generation 9</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        n = len(nums)
        # After sorting: pick pairs (small, large) as (nums[0],nums[1]),(nums[2],nums[3]),...
        # Bob takes the larger first, then Alice takes the smaller.
        res = []
        for i in range(0, n, 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])
            res.append(nums[i])
        return res
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 10</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        arr = []
        # take pairs from smallest to largest:
        # for each pair [a, b], output [b, a]
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            arr.append(nums[i + 1])
            arr.append(nums[i])
        return arr
```
```

### System Prompt

```text
You are an expert Python programmer. You will be given a question (problem specification) and will generate a correct Python program that matches the specification and passes all tests.
```

### User Prompt

```text
Before solving, consider these reusable insights from prior analysis:

  1. Sort + Pairwise Round Simulation: After sorting, the game’s behavior depends only on repeatedly removing the smallest two remaining elements per round and outputting them in the round’s append order.
     Sort the array so “remove minimum” becomes predictable as taking consecutive smallest elements. Then process the sorted array in pairs corresponding to each round’s two removals, appending in the exact order defined by who records first and who records second. This avoids trying to reason about the original unsorted dynamics.
  2. Preserve Interleaving Order from Turn Rules: When output order is determined by alternating turns, you must interleave results per round rather than group by index ranges.
     Even if you pick the correct multiset of values, producing them in the wrong global sequence fails. Track the exact append sequence within each round (who appends first) and mirror that by appending two values immediately as a block (with the correct internal order) before moving to the next pair.
  3. Validate Index-Mapping Against the Round Timeline: Complex index formulas should be checked by mapping them back to the per-round removal/append timeline to prevent subtle ordering errors.
     If you choose a direct indexing approach (writing into a preallocated result array), verify that each mapped position corresponds to the same element’s removal order and append order within its round. A quick timeline check—“first removal by Alice, second removal by Bob, then Bob appends before Alice”—catches off-by-one and swapping mistakes that grouping-based logic may miss.
  4. Use Direct Simulation When Abstractions Are Risky: If deriving a closed-form transformation risks ordering mistakes, a direct simulation with a priority structure can be more reliable.
     A min-heap (or equivalent) cleanly models “repeatedly remove current minimum” without relying on fragile index reasoning. This approach works especially well when the operation sequence is fixed (e.g., remove twice per round and append in a known order), providing a correctness baseline for comparison.

Apply these insights to the problem below.

### Question:
You are given a 0-indexed integer array nums of even length and there is also an empty array arr. Alice and Bob decided to play a game where in every round Alice and Bob will do one move. The rules of the game are as follows:

Every round, first Alice will remove the minimum element from nums, and then Bob does the same.
Now, first Bob will append the removed element in the array arr, and then Alice does the same.
The game continues until nums becomes empty.

Return the resulting array arr.
 
Example 1:

Input: nums = [5,4,2,3]
Output: [3,2,5,4]
Explanation: In round one, first Alice removes 2 and then Bob removes 3. Then in arr firstly Bob appends 3 and then Alice appends 2. So arr = [3,2].
At the begining of round two, nums = [5,4]. Now, first Alice removes 4 and then Bob removes 5. Then both append in arr which becomes [3,2,5,4].

Example 2:

Input: nums = [2,5]
Output: [5,2]
Explanation: In round one, first Alice removes 2 and then Bob removes 5. Then in arr firstly Bob appends and then Alice appends. So arr = [5,2].

 
Constraints:

1 <= nums.length <= 100
1 <= nums[i] <= 100
nums.length % 2 == 0

### Format: You will use the following starter code to write the solution to the problem and enclose your code within delimiters.
```python
class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        
```

### Answer: (use the provided format with backticks)
```

#### Outputs

<details>
<summary>Augmented generation 1</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List
import heapq

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        # Simulate via a min-heap:
        # Each round: Alice pops min, Bob pops next min.
        # Then Bob appends first, then Alice.
        h = nums[:]
        heapq.heapify(h)
        res = []
        while h:
            a = heapq.heappop(h)  # Alice's removal (min)
            b = heapq.heappop(h)  # Bob's removal (next min)
            res.append(b)        # Bob appends first
            res.append(a)        # Alice appends second
        return res
```
```

from typing import List
import heapq

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        # Min-heap simulation of:
        # each round: Alice pops min, Bob pops next min
        # append order: Bob's popped value first, then Alice's.
        heapq.heapify(nums)
        res = []
        while nums:
            alice = heapq.heappop(nums)
            bob = heapq.heappop(nums)
            res.append(bob)
            res.append(alice)
        return res

from typing import List
import heapq

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        # Simulate exactly using a min-heap:
        # Each round: Alice pops min, Bob pops next min; then Bob appends first, then Alice.
        h = nums[:]
        heapq.heapify(h)
        res = []
        while h:
            a = heapq.heappop(h)  # Alice removes minimum
            b = heapq.heappop(h)  # Bob removes new minimum
            res.append(b)         # Bob appends first
            res.append(a)         # Alice appends second
        return res
```

        # Use a min-heap to repeatedly remove the current minimum.
        # Each round: Alice pops min, Bob pops min; then Bob appends first, then Alice.
        h = nums[:]
        heapq.heapify(h)

        res = []
        while h:
            alice = heapq.heappop(h)
            bob = heapq.heappop(h)

from typing import List
import heapq

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        # Simulation with a min-heap:
        # Each round: Alice removes min, then Bob removes new min.
        # Then arr append order: Bob's removed first, then Alice's.
        heapq.heapify(nums)
        arr = []
        while nums:
            a = heapq.heappop(nums)  # Alice removes min
            b = heapq.heappop(nums)  # Bob removes next min
            arr.append(b)            # Bob appends first
            arr.append(a)            # Alice appends next
        return arr

<summary>Augmented generation 9</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        nums.sort()
        # In each round: Alice removes smallest, Bob removes next smallest.
        # Append order per round: Bob's removed value first, then Alice's.
        res = []
        for i in range(0, len(nums), 2):
            res.append(nums[i + 1])  # Bob's removal (second smallest in the pair)
            res.append(nums[i])      # Alice's removal (smallest in the pair)
        return res
```
```

</details>

<summary>Augmented generation 10</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List
import heapq

class Solution:
    def numberGame(self, nums: List[int]) -> List[int]:
        # Min-heap simulation (removes current minimum each step)
        heap = nums[:]
        heapq.heapify(heap)

        arr = []
        while heap:
            # Alice removes min
            a = heapq.heappop(heap)
            # Bob removes next min
            b = heapq.heappop(heap)
            # Bob appends first, then Alice
            arr.append(b)
            arr.append(a)

        return arr
```
